10.1016/j.jmp.2016.02.003
Duncan Luce is well-known for contributions to both psychology and economics. His work in psychology is well-known to the mathematical psychology community. Less so is his work in economics. The article by Jessie and Saari in this volume summarizes its importance for economic choice and game theory. He has also made major contributions to economic decision theory. The most relevant of these to ideas presented in this article are the three articles discussed below published economic journals and his remarkable book,  Utility of Gains and Losses,  ( Luce, 2000 ).

Luce was interested in both rational and irrational aspects of human behavior. The rational is the focus of economics. The irrational arises in ordinary human decision behavior. Economics theory of preference assumes the normative condition of transitivity of indifference. This is, as  Luce (1956)  points out, “contrary to experience and that utility is not perfectly discriminable”. In  Luce (1956)  he invents and formally axiomatizes the concept of “semiorder” and shows that it captures the classical psychological notion of “just noticeable difference”. He then uses semiorders, which has an intransitive indifference relation, to describe preference.  Luce (1956)  notes, 
                         In applications where a real-valued order-preserving utility function is postulated, a maximization principle is almost always employed which states in effect that a rational being will respond to any finite difference in utility, however small. It is, of course, false that people behave in this manner, but it is argued that this is one of the prices of a simple idealized theory. Nevertheless, some workers have continued to be uncomfortable, for it is clear that the idealized theory cannot exhibit certain possibly important phenomena. Possibly this is not a concern if all the applications are actually treated as normative, though even that is doubtful.

The research presented here uses several ideas inspired by Luce’s utility research. In particular,  
                         
                            • 
                            Luce tended to separate his axiomatizations into rational and non-rational components, trying to minimize the non-rational contribution. In the research presented here, the non-rational is confined to the cognitive interpretation of presented individual gambles. The psychological processes for evaluating and comparing the utilities of the  cognitive interpretations  of the presented gambles are modeled through rational principles. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Luce and Krantz (1971)  developed a version of  Savage (1954)  subjective expected utility theory that vastly reduced the number of decisions needed while simultaneously generalizing the theory. From an economic point of view, Luce’s and Krantz’s theory—like Savage’s—is normative. This article employs a version of their representation theorem to justify the normativeness of the major model used in this article. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            Following a large experimental literature on losses,  Luce (1991)  considered it important to separate gambles based on positive outcomes, from those based on negative ones, from those base on mixes of positive and negative outcomes. In his modeling, he assigns different probabilistic weighting functions depending on signs of the utilities (positive, negative, zero) and their relative sizes. In the research presented here, the modeling for similar situations is based on a radically different interpretation. The equations describing utilities are very similar for Luce’s model and the one described later in this article. The principal difference that this article’s model has a finitely additive probability function for events instead of weighting functions, making it much more rational- and normative-like.

Luce tended to separate his axiomatizations into rational and non-rational components, trying to minimize the non-rational contribution. In the research presented here, the non-rational is confined to the cognitive interpretation of presented individual gambles. The psychological processes for evaluating and comparing the utilities of the  cognitive interpretations  of the presented gambles are modeled through rational principles.

Luce and Krantz (1971)  developed a version of  Savage (1954)  subjective expected utility theory that vastly reduced the number of decisions needed while simultaneously generalizing the theory. From an economic point of view, Luce’s and Krantz’s theory—like Savage’s—is normative. This article employs a version of their representation theorem to justify the normativeness of the major model used in this article.

Following a large experimental literature on losses,  Luce (1991)  considered it important to separate gambles based on positive outcomes, from those based on negative ones, from those base on mixes of positive and negative outcomes. In his modeling, he assigns different probabilistic weighting functions depending on signs of the utilities (positive, negative, zero) and their relative sizes. In the research presented here, the modeling for similar situations is based on a radically different interpretation. The equations describing utilities are very similar for Luce’s model and the one described later in this article. The principal difference that this article’s model has a finitely additive probability function for events instead of weighting functions, making it much more rational- and normative-like.

Subjective Expected Utility Theory,  or  SEU  for short, has been used as the standard theory for rational decision making throughout science. The adequacy of SEU has been questioned many times, generally through purported counterexamples such as those provided by  Allais (1953)  and  Ellsberg (1961) . Other theories have been developed to incorporate these or other empirical or theoretical objections to SEU, such as the psychologically based Prospect Theory of  Tversky and Kahneman (1974)  and various economic and psychological theories that it motivated. These theories employ SEU’s primitive concepts, or conditional variants of them (e.g.,   Luce & Krantz, 1971 ), or those with additional primitive utility constants for status quo, zero value, or aspiration level, sometimes with a joint receipt operator on outcomes (e.g.   Luce, 2000 ).

The present article takes an entirely different approach to utility theory. It takes SEU as the gold standard for rationality, and expands it by introducing new primitive concepts for emotion and other kind of subjective modes that can influence decision making. The article’s goal is threefold:  
                         
                            (1) 
                            Expand SEU in a manner that incorporates these subjective modes, 
                         
                         
                            (2) 
                            investigate the normativeness and rationality of the expansion, and 
                         
                         
                            (3) 
                            show that the expansion is applicable to important issues in decision theory. 
                         
                       The expansion is called  multimode utility theory,  or  MUT  for short.

Expand SEU in a manner that incorporates these subjective modes,

investigate the normativeness and rationality of the expansion, and

show that the expansion is applicable to important issues in decision theory.

As an example of the kind of phenomena dealt with in MUT, consider the following experimental result of  Rottenstreich and Hsee (2001) , which is described in more detail in Section   5 : In the certainty condition of their experiment, one set of participants were asked to imagine that they could receive either “the opportunity to meet and kiss your favorite movie star” for sure or $50 in cash for sure. In the low probability condition, another set of participants were asked to imagine that they could take part in either a lottery offering a 1% chance of winning “the opportunity to meet and kiss your favorite movie star” or a lottery offering a 1% chance of winning $50 in cash. In the certainty condition, 70% of participants preferred the cash over the kiss, but in the low-probability condition, 65% of participants preferred the kiss lottery over the cash lottery. This data not only violates SEU but the large class of utility models that assume a fundamental axiom of measurement theory called “conjoint independence” holding between probabilities and utilities of positive outcomes. (See Chapter 6 of  Krantz, Luce, Suppes, & Tversky, 1971 , for formal definitions and discussion of conjoint independence, which they call “independence”.) In MUT, affect can influence probabilistic weights in manners that can accommodate results like those from the “kiss your favorite movie star” experiment.

MUT is designed to better model subjectivity. In SEU, subjectivity is modeled using a subjective probability function on events within a lottery and a subjective utility function on outcomes within a lottery. These events and outcomes are objective, and the events form a boolean algebra. However, in MUT the events used by the decision maker in making decisions are subjective and are treated in a different manner than objective events; because of this, they will not necessarily form a boolean algebra.

It is this difference in algebras that allows for the introduction of modes while obtaining most of the basic concepts inherent in SEU. Instead of a boolean algebra of events, MUT employs an algebra of open sets from a topology, called throughout this article, a  topological algebra of events.

Boolean algebras correspond to classical propositional logics. Topological algebras of events correspond to a special case of a different but well-studied, important propositional logic called  intuitionistic propositional logic.  Topological algebras of events contain the minimal algebraic structure that is needed for defining a rich and applicable probability calculus.

Intuitionistic propositional logic has many interpretations. Its use here has nothing to do with the concept of “intuition” or its original interpretation as used in the foundations of mathematics: Here it is used as an algebra that generalizes the classical concept of “event space”, while remaining friendly to probability theory.

There has been only limited theoretical or applied research using probability theories based on topological algebras of events or intuitionistic logics. The following are some examples that are relevant for the concerns of this article.  
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Narens (2007, 2009)  used topological algebras of events to provide a foundation for the psychological theory of probability estimation known in literature as  Support Theory.  The 2009 article also provides a foundation and theory for the Kahneman and Tversky heuristic of availability. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Weatherson (2003)  formulated the Dutch Book argument for the rationality of probability functions defined on intuitionistic logics. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Shin (1993)  showed intuitionistic logic provided a natural foundation for the notion of common knowledge in game theory. 
                         
                         
                            • 
                            
                               Narens (2005)  showed topological algebras of events provided a superior philosophic foundation than boolean algebras for a probability theory in which all events are empirical.

Narens (2007, 2009)  used topological algebras of events to provide a foundation for the psychological theory of probability estimation known in literature as  Support Theory.  The 2009 article also provides a foundation and theory for the Kahneman and Tversky heuristic of availability.

Weatherson (2003)  formulated the Dutch Book argument for the rationality of probability functions defined on intuitionistic logics.

Shin (1993)  showed intuitionistic logic provided a natural foundation for the notion of common knowledge in game theory.

Narens (2005)  showed topological algebras of events provided a superior philosophic foundation than boolean algebras for a probability theory in which all events are empirical.

This article contains a few illustrative examples of multimode utility theory. One involves lotteries containing catastrophic events.  Chichilnisky (2009)  proposed a novel model for evaluating the utility of such lotteries. In her model, catastrophic events are rare and induce fear in the decision maker. (Her article is titled, “The Topology of Fear”.) She argues that standard economic theory produces near zero utility for the portion of the lottery involving catastrophic outcomes. In fact, the utility for these can be so close to zero in the standard theory, that, for practical purposes, they can be ignored in making practical decisions, which she argues is a state of affairs that runs counter to intuition. In Section   5 , Chichilnisky’s theory is discussed, and it is shown that MUT captures the essential qualitative features of her theory.

MUT assumes a decision maker who enters into various decision states—called  modes —that influence her subjective probabilities. The modes are determined by such factors as the decision maker’s current emotion, the utility of an outcome of a lottery under consideration, a lottery’s perceived risk, etc.

In traditional utility theories and their generalizations, events and the determination of their occurrence/non-occurrence are objective. A consequence of this form of objectivity is that the events form a boolean space. Also, in such utility theories, the decision maker’s subjective probability function is the only means for capturing the subjectivity inherent in her evaluation of events. In contrast, in MUT, the choice of the event space, which in general is non-boolean, together with the decision maker’s subjective probability function capture the subjectivity inherent in the evaluation of events as well as the subjectivity in the evaluation of their occurrence/non-occurrence. Because of this, the specification of MUT’s event space and its key mathematical properties are central to this article’s approach to decision theory: They allow for the more realistic modeling of subjectivity than is possible for boolean event spaces.

In MUT, it is the decision maker’s  interpretations  of the events appearing in lotteries that form the domain for her subjective probability function, instead of, like in SEU, the events. Because an event’s interpretation varies with mode, an event may have many interpretations. MUT characterizes interpretations as open sets from a topology. They together with algebraic operations of set-theoretic union, set-theoretic intersection, and a new kind of complementation operation appropriate for open sets form MUT’s event space. This event space is boolean only in extremely degenerate situations. This is because there is almost always at least one open set whose set-theoretic complement is not open.

MUT models the multiple subjective interpretations of an event in a manner so that the resulting decision theory leaves intact many of the core features of standard theories for rational decision making. MUT achieves this by through the use of boundaries of open sets.

In most uses of standard probability theory, the subsets of the boundaries of open sets have probability 0. From a decision standpoint, this means that for such uses, it is not of practical importance to distinguish an open set

Treating multiple interpretations as events will cause difficulties unless care is taken to have the multiple interpretations of the same event be appropriately related and appropriately assigned probabilities. This is accomplished in MUT through the use of basic topological concepts.

Boundaries considered as topological concepts can be very different than boundaries considered as geometric concepts. For example, in 2-dimensional euclidean geometry, the boundary of a nonempty open set is “thin” in the sense that it has no area. The boundary can be made “thick” by using a topology that is different from the one generated through euclidean geometry. The following is an example.                        In boolean algebras each event has exactly one event that is its pseudo complement—its complement. Thus situations involving an event that is a pseudo complement of more than one event cannot be modeled by boolean algebras.

There are many concepts of rationality involving lotteries. For the purposes of this article, the ones of interest have in common that the decision maker chooses a lottery that has greatest utility for her. The concepts differ in restrictions they put on the computation of utility. Some involve logical rationality, which requires two lotteries that logically describe the same situation have the same utility. For these, the amount of resources and the available computational power used to compute the logical equivalence are not issues. Others require a form of psychological rationality that has a decision maker with limited resources and processing power for computing the utility of lotteries. Nevertheless, the decision maker carries out the computation in a rational way given those limitations. An economic concept of rationality requires the decision maker to be coherent in her economic decisions; that is, she will not sell a set of lotteries at a given price that can be rearranged into a different, logically equivalent set of lotteries that she will purchase at a higher price. Economic rationality implies logical rationality if for each lottery  
                         L 
                      , the decision maker’s buying price for  
                         L 
                       is the same as her selling price for  
                         L 
                      .

MUT models often do not satisfy logical rationality. This is not surprising, because logical rationality is formulated in terms of objective lotteries and MUT models are formulated in terms of subjective interpretations of lotteries. Because of the subjectivity inherent in MUT, psychological rationality is the most appropriate kind of rationality concept for MUT. The coherence ideas inherent in economic rationality can be reformulated so that they apply to interpretations of lotteries instead of to lotteries. When this is done, there is a special form of MUT called DSEU for which the reformulated coherence holds. DSEU has many links with rationality and the SEU model of decision making used in economics and throughout science, and this article explores several of these links.

This section provides some of the mathematical terminology and conventions used throughout this article.

This article generalizes the classical event structure of probability theory to in order to formulate new concepts of “normativeness”. This is done by changing the concept of complementation used in boolean algebras to a more general concept. The algebraic structure of sets with the more general complementation concept is called a “pseudo complement distributive algebra of events”.

In probability theory based on Lebesgue and related measures, the probability of the boundary of an open set is 0. But the uses of probability theory in this article are more general in that

(i) 
                            they allow for topologies of other kinds than those generally considered in measure theory, and 
                         
                         
                            (ii) 
                            they use probability functions that may assign probabilities >0 to subsets of boundaries of open sets—even in some instances where the open sets are from Euclidean space. 
                         
                       Also the probability theory developed in this article is finitely additive probability, which includes the traditional probability theory that is  
                         σ 
                      -additive as a special case.

they allow for topologies of other kinds than those generally considered in measure theory, and

they use probability functions that may assign probabilities >0 to subsets of boundaries of open sets—even in some instances where the open sets are from Euclidean space.

                        onto a boolean algebra of sets. In terms of lattice theory,  
                         P 
                        is a boolean lattice, because it can be shown to be a complemented, distributive lattice (Theorem 4 of  Narens, 2005 ; Theorem 9.2 of  Narens, 2007 ).

“Multimode utility theory”, or “MUT” for short, is based in the idea that the decision maker is given a description of a lottery in the outside world that she interprets subjectively as a lottery. A given description can have multiple subjective interpretations. The decision maker assigns utility values to each of these interpretations. “Descriptive subjective expected utility theory”, or “DSEU” for short, is a special MUT model designed to emphasize a  subjective version  of rationality while incorporating in psychological behavior that may violate properties of subjective expected utility theory, SEU. It is a special case of another MUT model GDSEU (“Generalized DSEU”) that uses a formula for computing utility that has a form similar to that of SEU. Formal descriptions of DSEU and GDSEU are given later in this section after the definitions and presentations of some preliminary concepts.

Descriptions of lotteries and their parts are put in boldface to distinguish them from their interpretations. Thus a description  
                         
                            L 
                         
                       of a lottery has a subjective interpretation  
                         L 
                      , called its  subjective lottery,  as well as its objective interpretation as a lottery  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               ⋆ 
                            
                         
                       in the outside world, called its  objective lottery.  As discussed later, subjective interpretations can vary with context, and thus  
                         
                            L 
                         
                       may describe many different subjective lotteries. For the purposes of this article, it is assumed that it only describes one objective lottery.

MUT allows the decision maker to sometimes enter into different kinds of decision modes when determining a utility of a lottery. These modes arise in various ways. For example, the utilities of different parts of a lottery may arise from different sources, e.g., different experts or different methodologies, and the decision maker must combine these “modes” to into a single utility for the lottery. Or a decision maker can enter into different psychological modes in judging various parts of a lottery, e.g., in a “fearful mode” when judging a part of a lottery involving a catastrophic outcome or a catastrophic event, and an “emotionally neutral” mode otherwise. The fearful mode could consist of various kinds or degrees of fearfulness, each producing its own distinctive impact the decision maker’s utility evaluation.

In MUT, the utility of a lottery  
                         L 
                       depends on the of subjective interpretations of the outcomes in  
                         L 
                       and on the conditional probabilities of the interpretations of events in  
                         L 
                      . The conditional probabilities depend on mode the decision maker is in while evaluating the interpretation of the events. In the case where the MUT model is a DESU model, an outcome  
                         c 
                       may be viewed as triggering the mode used by the decision maker in evaluating the utility of a term  
                         c 
                         , 
                         A 
                       of a lottery, for example, in evaluating the term  
                         c 
                         , 
                         A 
                       when  
                         c 
                       is catastrophic and puts her into fearful mode, while evaluating the term  
                         d 
                         , 
                         A 
                       for non-catastrophic  
                         d 
                       puts her into a non-fearful mode.

The decision maker’s subjective probability judgment for an event depends on the mode in which the judgment is made. In modeling this, descriptions of the event and the lotteries containing the event are used. This provides a means for characterizing the effects of changes of mode on the decision maker’s interpretations of events and their judged subjective probabilities. Modeling this by allowing the “interpretation” be an interpretation of a description permits for a smooth and rigorous treatment of “interpretation”. A related use of descriptions has been successfully employed in cognitive psychology to model the impact of framing on probability judgments (e.g.,   Rottenstreich & Tversky, 1997; Tversky & Koehler, 1994 ).

                      . Analogous terminology holds for  lottery outcome description.  The only descriptions used by theories or models in this article are descriptions of lotteries, events, and outcomes.

Modes and interpretations

Throughout this article,  
                         OUT 
                       denotes the decision maker’s set of outcomes and  
                         M 
                       denotes the decision maker’s set of modes. One mode that is sometimes in  
                         M 
                       is called the  rational mode  and is denoted by  
                         R 
                      . It has special properties that are described later. Unless otherwise stated, it is assumed that  
                         M 
                       has at least two elements.

Probability and the DESU model

Throughout this article,  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       is a function from  
                         A 
                       into the real interval [0,1] with the following properties for all  
                         B 
                       and  
                         C 
                       in  
                         A

                            (1) 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     X

                                     B

                                  0 
                                  
                                  iff 
                                  
                                  B

                       Properties (1) and (2) say  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       is a finitely additive probability function on the pseudo complemented distributive algebra  
                         A 
                      . Because  
                         A 
                       is not necessarily a boolean algebra, the concept of a probability function has changed slightly: The proposition that for all  
                         B 
                       and  
                         C 
                       in the algebra,  
                         
                            (1) 
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  B 
                                  ∪ 
                                  C 
                                  )

                                  B

                                  C

                               − 
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  B 
                                  ∩ 
                                  C 
                                  ) 
                               
                               , 
                            
                         
                       captures the kind of finite additivity that in boolean algebras is usually expressed as follows: For all  
                         B 
                       and  
                         C 
                       in the algebra, if  
                         B 
                         ∩ 
                         C 
                         = 
                         ∅ 
                      , then  
                         
                            (2) 
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  B 
                                  ∪ 
                                  C 
                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  B 
                                  ) 
                               
                               + 
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  C 
                                  ) 
                               
                               . 
                            
                         
                       Because boolean algebras are relatively complemented, Eqs.  (1) and (2)  are logically equivalent for boolean algebras. For topological algebras of events, however, they are not logically equivalent: Eq.  (1) , which implies Eq.  (2) , is a more powerful and restrictive condition than Eq.  (2) .

P 
                                  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     X 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                  =

P 
                                  
                                  
                                     (

Property (3) says that  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       has no non-trivial events of probability 0. This assumption is introduced for convenience to simplify definitions and statements of results. The theory easily extends to  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       having non-trivial events of probability 0. Such an extension will be made when circumstances require it.

P 
                         
                       is used to formulate a probabilistic aspect that hold in some MUT models, e.g., in the DSEU model formulated below. The finite additivity of  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       is assumed because a principal intent of this article is to extend the main ideas of SEU to a decision maker with shifting modes, and finite additivity (instead of  
                         σ 
                      -additivity) is a consequence of some standard axiomatizations of SEU. The multimode aspect of MUT with the probability function  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       can account for some forms of non-additivity that appear in the empirical, psychological literature. However, for a more encompassing form of descriptive multimode modeling, the assumption that  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       is a probability function would need to be modified.

                                                 A 
                                              
                                           
                                           
                                              1 
                                           
                                        
                                        )

                                     ⋯ 
                                     +

The modeling of all of the lottery descriptions through MUT, the above assumptions about  
                         
                            P 
                         
                      , and Eq.  (4)  is called the  General Descriptive Subjective Expected Utility Model  or  GDSEU  for short. It is very general in the sense that it does not specify what  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       depends on. As such, to make GDSEU useful,  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       must be further specified. This is done in the  Descriptive Subjective Expected Utility  model or  DSEU  model for short, which is the main focus of this article. A DSEU model is a GDSEU model that requires that  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       to be a function of  
                         
                            
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               
                                  A 
                               
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                      . As a result,  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       can depend  
                         
                            
                               
                                  a 
                               
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                       as well as  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                      . This allows for the formulation of modeling of concepts that are not formulable in SEU. For example, various concepts involving risk in SEU are formulated in terms of properties of a function  
                         F 
                       that relates exogenous measurement of outcomes to their utilities, for example, when  
                         u 
                         
                            ( 
                            a 
                            ) 
                         
                         = 
                         F 
                         
                            ( 
                            $ 
                            a 
                            ) 
                         
                      , where  
                         $ 
                         a 
                       is the market price of  
                         a 
                       in US dollars. The Arrow–Pratt measure of absolute risk-aversion is an example. By having subjective probabilities partially depend on utilities—as in DSEU—additional dimensions of risk can be formulated, and their impact on the utility of lotteries can be analyzed. Examples are presented later in this article. Sometimes the additional dimensions alone are sufficient for explaining phenomena, that is, the use of exogenous decision concepts can be avoided.

Theorem 1  at the end of Section   10  shows that under standard background assumptions, a DSEU model can be imbedded in a SEU model generated by the utilities of the outcomes and the boolean algebra generated by  
                         A 
                      . This means that many  subjective  coherences based on SEU principles extends to DSEU. A richer, and more applicable, theory results by adding further assumptions about modes, and examples of this is presented throughout the remainder of the article.

It is important to keep in mind that MUT subsumes many kinds of models such as the GDSEU models and that DSEU models are a special case of GDSEU models.

There are several ways to use the concepts of “objective utility” and “objective interpretation” in lottery decision making. The one that applies best for this article’s considerations involving DSEU and rationality is to assume that the objective utility of a lottery as the utility the decision maker would assign to the lottery if she were in a rational mode while judging the lottery.                        An approach to upper and lower probabilities based on a related idea is presented in Section   7 . 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         The basic idea of semantic coherence is that mode (i.e., context) determines the interpretation of a description of an event and that these interpretations are related through double pseudo complementation.  Ilin and Zhang (2014)  develops a modeling technique where each context produces a topology on the same underlying topological space. The topologies are organized into a lattice and upper and lower probabilities for events are constructed using this lattice.

Complex modes and submodes

MUT involves decisions where the decision maker changes modes during calculating the utility of lotteries, causing her interpretations of events to fluctuate with mode. In MUT, related modes are collected together to form a  complex mode,  so that fluctuations within the complex mode can be viewed as variations of one another. The modes within a complex mode  
                         C 
                       are called  submodes  of  
                         C 
                      . An example is several fear modes, with related modes varying from weak fear to strong fear. When the decision maker is in the complex fear mode,  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               F 
                            
                         
                      , then she is in one of its submodes. Appropriate comparisons of the subjective probabilities across submodes of  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               F 
                            
                         
                       can yield important modeling information about the impact the subjective intensity of fear on decision making.

For this scheme of complex modes and submodes to be work in multimode utility theory, the complex modes must be constrained by coherence considerations. For example, consider the description of a two outcome lottery  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               a 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               A 
                            
                            ; 
                            
                               b 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               B 
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       with the decision maker in complex mode  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       when evaluating the lottery event description  
                         
                            A 
                         
                      , and in complex mode  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       when evaluating the lottery event description  
                         
                            B 
                         
                      . Then the interpretations of  
                         
                            A 
                         
                       in any submode of  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       and of  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       in any submode of  
                         
                            
                               C 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       must be disjoint to keep them coherent within the concept of “lottery”, because, to have the subjective interpretation of the description of the lottery to act like a lottery, the events within the subjectively interpreted lottery must be disjoint. The following definitions of “complex mode” and “submode” provide a sound footing for multimode utility involving applications with many complex modes containing many submodes.

In multimode applications, the set of modes  
                         M

In SEU, the choice of the underlying boolean algebra determines the event structure. Sometimes the choice is critical, e.g., a finite versus an infinite algebra, or a countably additive versus finitely additive algebra. Similarly in MUT, the choice of the topology is critical, sometimes for the same kind of concerns as SEU involving the kind of event structure needed, for example, for the existence of the appropriate kind of probability function under consideration, and other times for concerns involving the structure of the set of modes,  
                         M 
                      , for example, for the existence of appropriate kinds of complex modes. Fortunately, both boolean algebras and pseudo complemented distributive algebras are highly flexible.

                       Eq.  (11)  is an example of  superadditivity , which happens when the sum of probabilities of disjoint events is < the probability of their union. When the sum of probabilities of disjoint events is > the probability of their union, it is called  subadditivity. One common finding in many empirical studies is the  S-shaped effect.  It is a fundamental property of many theories of decision making, in particular, Prospect Theory ( Kahneman & Tversky, 1979 ) and its generalizations/modifications.                       . In the literature, the S-shaped effect is shown to hold for both positive and negative outcomes in risky lotteries. 
                         4 
                      
                      
                         4 
                         A  risky  lottery is a lottery where the probabilities are objective and given. SEU and DSEU lotteries are lotteries with  uncertain  events. In SEU and DSEU lotteries, the probabilities are not given, but are derived from the decision maker’s behavior. Because of this, such probabilities are viewed as subjective rather than objective. (In the literature, risky gambles are often modeled using subjective decision weights that are functions of the lottery’s objective probabilities.)

Various studies in the literature, (e.g., see  Rottenstreich & Hsee, 2001 , for citations) have shown that affect rich outcomes heighten the S-Shape Effect when compared to affect poor outcomes. Rottenstreich & Hsee write,  
                         Weighting functions will be more S-shaped for lotteries involving affect-rich than affect-poor outcomes. That is, people will be more sensitive to departures from impossibility and certainty but less sensitive to intermediate probability variations for affect-rich outcomes. We corroborated this prediction by observing probability-outcome interactions: An affect-poor prize was preferred over an affect-rich prize under certainty, but the direction of preference reversed under low probability. We suggest that the assumption of probability-outcome independence, adopted by both expected-utility and prospect theory, may hold across outcomes of different monetary values, but not different affective values. (p. 185)

The kissing study mentioned near the beginning of this article is one example. Its details are as follows:  
                         Forty Rice University undergraduates completed a short questionnaire included in a packet of unrelated questionnaires. The study consisted of two between-subjects probability conditions: certainty and low probability. Participants in the certainty condition  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  n 
                                  = 
                                  20 
                                  ) 
                               
                             were asked to imagine that they could receive either “the opportunity to meet and kiss your favorite movie star” or $50 in cash These participants indicated which of the two prizes they preferred. Participants in the low-probability condition  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  n 
                                  = 
                                  20 
                                  ) 
                               
                             were asked to imagine that they could take part in either a lottery offering a 1% chance of winning “the opportunity to meet and kiss your favorite movie star” or a lottery offering a 1% chance of winning $50 in cash. These participants indicated which lottery they preferred to play. 
                         The study revealed a dramatic probability-outcome interaction. In the certainty condition, 70% of participants preferred the cash over the kiss, but in the low-probability condition, 65% of participants preferred the kiss lottery over the cash lottery,  
                               
                                  
                                     χ 
                                  
                                  
                                     2

                                  ) 
                               
                               = 
                               4.91

                               p 
                               < 
                               0.05 
                            . ( Rottenstreich & Hsee, 2001 , p. 186–187)

For risky lotteries, SEU and the S-Shaped Effect are inconsistent. However, this is not the case for DSEU, because DSEU puts no requirement on how

                       and  
                         
                            B 
                         
                       and mode

In summary, it is possible add the S-Shaped Effect and other shaped effects to DSEU without introducing conflicts with other DSEU principles.

Fear induced through catastrophes

Chichilnisky (2009)  presents a generalization of classical subjective expected utility theory that emphasizes the special role that catastrophes play in human decision processes. Her intent is “to define rational behavior more broadly, and more in tune with the way humans behave”. She provides the following two illustrations of the kind of problem encountered in expected utility theory that her generalization is designed to avoid.  
                         As an illustration consider the rule of thumb “maximize expected utility while minimizing the worst losses in the event of a catastrophe”. This rule is inconsistent with expected utility. Therefore any observer that anticipates expected utility optimization will be disappointed, and will believe that there is irrationality at stake. But this is not true. The rule is rational once we take into account rational responses to extreme events. It is consistent with what people do on an everyday basis, and with the experimental evidence on “jump–diffusion processes” and “heavy tails” distributions that are not well explained by standard theory.  (pg. 805) 
                         
                         …The following example is from  Arrow (1971, pp. 48–49) , see also  Chichilnisky (2009, pp. 257–258) . If a is an action that involves receiving one cent, b is another that involves receiving zero cents, and c is a third action involving receiving one cent and facing a small probability of death, Arrow’s Monotone Continuity requires that the third action involving death and one cent should be preferred to the second involving zero cents when the probability of death is small enough. Even Kenneth Arrow says of his requirement “this may sound outrageous at first blush” ( Arrow, 1971 ) … In other cases, there may be a small enough probability that the lottery involving death may be acceptable. It all depends on what are the other outcomes involved. For example, if instead of one cent and zero cents one considers one billion dollars and zero cents—as Arrow suggests in  Arrow (1971) —under certain conditions one may be willing to take the lottery that involves a small probability of death and one billion dollars over the one that offers half a cent. More to the point, a small probability of death caused by a medicine that can cure an incurable cancer may be preferable to no cure. This seems a reasonable solution to the issue that Arrow raises. Sometimes one is willing to take a risk with a small enough probability of a catastrophe, in other cases one is not. It all depends on what else is involved.  (pg. 812)

Chichilnisky formulates her theory in terms of  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       versus  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               ∞ 
                            
                         
                       measure spaces. For some of her ideas, properties of these spaces—particularly  
                         
                            
                               L 
                            
                            
                               ∞ 
                            
                         
                       and its dual space—are crucial. But her basic ideas about how fear changes evaluations of lotteries extend to DSEU, which uses a much wider variety of probabilistic spaces. In DSEU, the decision maker evaluates catastrophic events in fearful mode. The interpretation of probabilistic weights in DSEU as subjective conditional probabilities plays a major role in achieving the intent of defining “rational behavior more broadly, and more in tune with the way humans behave”. For the lottery description  
                         
                            L 
                         
                         = 
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     a 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  1

The framework presented for fear in this section can easily accommodate intermediate degrees of fearfulness by forming a complex fear mode with submodes differing in subjective intensity. A mechanism for modeling this has been presented in footnote 4.

SEU is generally taken as the rational model for individual decision making. It, however, has limited capabilities for modeling risk. It is somewhat controversial whether incorporations of concepts of risk that are outside of the SEU framework are “rational”. Instead of entering directly into this debate at this point, I would like to mention that the approach of this section expands the scope of SEU-like modeling in directions that may be useful in policy decisions involving catastrophic risks in real-world situations that find SEU unacceptable for political or moral or other reasons.

Hope

The concepts of “hope” and “fear” are often used in describing people’s behavior involving lotteries (e.g., see   Lopes, 1987 ), with hope increasing the subjective likelihoods of events yielding large gains and fear increasing the subjective likelihoods of events yielding large losses. The DSEU model for hope and fear also has similarities to sign dependent models of utility theory, with hope being identified with positive outcomes, and fear with negative ones. The use of the modes of hope and fear permits the concepts of “positive” and “negative” to be attached to outcomes, allowing for a model that functionally captures a version of sign dependent utility theory with an interval scale of utilities. This model has absolute concepts of “positive” (i.e., hope) and “negative” (i.e., fear) that are different from the sign dependent modeling for interval scales using the relative notions of “positive” and “negative” with respect to a reference element, and is different from the sign dependent modeling for ratio scales of “positive” and “negative” using an absolute zero element.

Just as in the modeling of catastrophes of the previous section, the theory can be modified so that events can have multiple degrees of hope and fear instead of just a single degree.

Alertness

In alertness  
                         M 
                       has two modes:  heightenly alert,  
                      
                         H 
                         
                         A 
                       and  normally alert,  
                      
                         N 
                         
                         A 
                      . Both of these apply to all lottery descriptions, and therefore to all lottery event descriptions. A lottery event description evaluated in the mode of heighten alertness is assumed to produce an increase in subjective conditional probability when compared to the decision maker’s normally alert mode. It is assume that for all lottery event descriptions  
                         
                            A 
                         
                      ,

As modeled in this and the previous section, the primary difference between hope and alertness resides in the interpretations of descriptions of events. In alertness modes, the decision maker will receive all outcomes she expects, and sometimes she will be surprised by being given an outcome she did not expect. In the hope mode, she sometimes may be disappointed, because her expected receipt of a positive outcome did not materialize, but she will never be surprised by receiving an outcome she did not expect.

Loss aversion

The idea for sign dependent utility models is that loss adverse decision makers treat losses different from gains.  Luce (1991)  provides various models for rank and sign-dependent utility for binary gambles. His binary gambles are partitions of the sure event. The following is simplest general sign dependent model for gambles involving money:

Comparison of Eq.  (16)  to Eqs.  (17)–(20)  show the two models to be very similar. The principal difference is that Eqs.  (17)–(20)  require much more additivity than Eq.  (16) , because  
                         
                            P 
                         
                       is required to be a probability function. This makes Eqs.  (17)–(20)  much more rational or normative-like than Eq.  (16) , and thus a better model than Eq.  (16)  for things such as public policy decisions when loss aversion is included as a priority. On the other hand, for laboratory studies of gambling with relatively small sums of money, Eq.  (16)  is likely to describe data better than Eqs.  (17)–(20) , because of the well-known failure of probabilistic additivity in related studies.

Note that the Luce model has four weighting functions, while the DSEU model has only just two probability functions. This allows the Luce model to provide finer distinctions to the modeling of binary gambles than is possible for the DSEU model. Such finer distinctions, if needed, can be incorporated into a multimode extension of the DSEU model with four modes.

Virtual states  are used to fatten up events to give them larger probabilities. Rather than considered or processed by the decision maker, they are a mathematical fiction used to account for changes in subjective probabilities of a lottery event description under change of mode. In contrast,  mirage states  are states that are entertained by the decision maker in certain modes. These states are impossible, but are not understood as such by the decision maker.

DSEU, as currently presented, will now be extended to include virtual and mirage states by making the following small adjustments to the theory. The main change has to do with the interpretation of  
                         X 
                      .

Up to now,  
                         X 
                       had two coincidental roles as:  
                         
                            (1) 
                            the universe of all states, and 
                         
                         
                            (2) 
                            the set of all states that cause some event in some lottery to occur objectively. 
                         
                       With the inclusion of virtual and mirage states, (1) and (2) differ, because (1) now contains virtual and mirage states, which are not  real states  of the universe, that is, they are not states that can cause some event to occur objectively, and (2) contains the real states of the universe and only the real states of the universe. Because of this, different symbols are used to represent (1) and (2):  
                         X 
                       for the universe containing all real, virtual, and mirage states of the universe, and  
                         S 
                       for set of real states of the universe.  
                         S 
                         ⊆ 
                         X 
                      , and it is the states in  
                         S 
                       that determine the outcomes of lotteries.  
                         X 
                      , as before, is called the  universe of states,  and  
                         S 
                       will be called the  real sure event.  The topology  
                         T 
                        is, as before, a topology with universe  
                         X 
                      . The naming convention governing descriptions and the set  
                         O 
                       of objective events need to be changed a little. The elements of  
                         O 
                       are now subsets of  
                         S 
                      , and if a lottery event description is needed for “the sure event”, then the description is  
                         
                            S 
                         
                       and not  
                         
                            X 
                         
                      .

the universe of all states, and

the set of all states that cause some event in some lottery to occur objectively.

                         E 
                       
                      is a nonempty set of mirage elements.  Interestingly, while the forms of subjectivity are very different in Cases 2 and 3, they have the same decision behaviors. First, the mirage elements impact probability in the same manner as virtual elements, because the addition of either kind of element to an event  
                         A 
                       produces an event with larger probability. Although the two cases’ subjective understandings of lottery event descriptions have different properties, these properties are not pragmatically displayed, because virtual states, by their nature, have no pragmatic consequences, and mirage states are never realized. Thus pragmatically, Cases 2 and 3 are in agreement for the decision maker and for the bookie as to whether the event  
                         A 
                       described by  
                         
                            A 
                         
                       occurred or did not occur. Case 3 decision makers, however, expect some circumstances for the occurrence of  
                         A 
                       that are not conceived by either the Case 2 decision maker or the bookie, but such circumstances have no pragmatic consequences, because they are never realized.

In summary, DSEU can be augmented to incorporate virtual and mirage states. The incorporation of these states allows for additional subjective interpretations of a lottery event description  
                         
                            A 
                         
                      . The additional interpretations can affect the size of the probability associated with description  
                         
                            A 
                         
                      , but they do not affect the decision maker’s belief about the actual occurrence of the event that  she  describes as  
                         
                            A 
                         
                      .

Rationality is an uncommonly controversial topic. Even its very definition is controversial.  Gilboa, Postlewaite, and Schmeidler (2009)  write,  
                         We reject the view that rationality is a clear-cut, binary notion that can be defined by a simple set of rules or axioms. There are various ingredients to rational choice. Some are of internal coherence, as captured by Savage’s axioms. Others have to do with external coherence with data and scientific reasoning. The question we should ask is not whether a particular decision is rational or not, but rather, whether a particular decision is more rational than another. And we should be prepared to have conflicts between the different demands of rationality. When such conflicts arise, compromises are called for. Sometimes we may relax our demands of internal consistency; at other times we may lower our standards of justifications for choices. But the quest for a single set of rules that will universally define the rational choice is misguided. (pp. 288–289) 
                       Nevertheless, SEU, exemplified by Savage’s axioms, has become the gold standard for rationality, especially in economics. For the purposes of the topics engaged in this article, its main weakness is its limited range of primitives, which limits its applicability. In particular, it has the decision maker operating only in one mode, a “rational mode”. This eliminates from consideration central issues like, What is rationality for a decision maker fluctuating among modes? Humans are such decision makers, especially when deciding matters of grave importance.

The approach of this article has been to expand SEU to a wider range of decision making situations, including ones where emotions and biases influence the decision making. To do this, some of SEU’s primitive concepts were altered and new primitive concepts were added. In particular, the event space was reduced from a boolean algebra to a pseudo complemented distributive algebra, and the set of lotteries that could enter into decisions was reduced. The new primitive concepts consisted of complex modes, submodes, descriptions of lotteries, mode dependent subjective and objective interpretations of descriptions, and virtual and mirage states. Their introduction expands the SEU setup to wider and more realistic situations of decision making. They also provide for the development of richer and useful theoretical concepts and consequentially for a richer theory of decision making.

Conceptually, the reduction from a boolean algebra to a pseudo complemented distributive algebra is not a reduction but an enhancement: Although they have fewer events than the boolean algebras they generate, topological algebras of events are mathematically richer than such boolean algebras, and multimode utility models exploit the extra mathematical richness. SEU models and multimode utility models differ in the range of lotteries considered. SEU models employ the widest range of lotteries formulable out of  
                         OUT 
                       and the boolean algebra generated by  
                         A 
                      . DSEU lottery descriptions, interpretations, and understandings apply only a subset of events from such boolean algebras, because they are limited by the choice of  
                         A 
                       and semantic and submode coherences. These coherences are designed to eliminate certain conflicting assignments of subjective probabilities in DSEU models in order that descriptions of lotteries remain subjectively interpretable and understandable as lotteries. I consider both of these coherences to be mild restrictions that are not likely to cause problems in reasonable applications of DSEU.

Conventional economic wisdom suggests that a decision maker whose utilities for lotteries fluctuate with mode should be economically irrational, that is, a Dutch Book or some other economic instrument should be able to be designed to reveal the decision maker’s irrationality. However, demonstrations of economic irrationality generally assume that the decision maker and the market or bookie are in agreement about the occurrence/nonoccurence of events, and this is not always the case with DSEU. Such disagreements are common enough in our society that institutions such as arbitration and contract law have evolved to deal with them. Of more direct, economic decision interest are cases where the decision maker and the market or bookie are in agreement. This is the focus of this subsection, which considers two forms of simple illustrative examples.

Case 1. The decision maker has two modes,  
                         
                            M 
                          
                         and  
                         
                            N 
                         
                         . These modes are not lottery driven, that is, they do not depend on descriptions of events, of outcomes, or of lotteries, and it is known that the decision maker has to alternate through these two modes with her utility function varying with mode. (Note that this is not a DSEU case.)  Let  
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                               
                                  M 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                               
                                  N 
                               
                            
                          be her utility functions in, respectively, modes  
                            M 
                          and  
                            N 
                         . Suppose she owns the lottery  
                            L 
                         , which has outcomes that are various amounts of dollars, and suppose  
                            a 
                            < 
                            b 
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  u 
                               
                               
                                  M 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               $ 
                               a 
                               ) 
                            
                            =

                                  u 
                               
                               
                                  N 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               $ 
                               b 
                               ) 
                            
                         . Normal rationality arguments applied to this situation would have the decision maker be willing, in mode  
                            M 
                          sell  
                            L 
                          for slightly more than  
                            $ 
                            a 
                         , in mode  
                            N 
                          be willing to buy  
                            L 
                          for slightly less than  
                            $ 
                            b 
                         . If the decision maker had such buying and selling strategies, then she would be economically irrational, because an arbitrageur could wait until she was in mode  
                            M 
                          and buy  
                            L 
                          at a price between  
                            $ 
                            a 
                          and  
                            $ 
                            b 
                         , and when she was in mode  
                            N 
                          sell it to her at a higher price, and repeat the process yielding a money pump. But there is no logical or even economic need for the decision maker to buy and sell  
                            L 
                          at any prices between  
                            $ 
                            a 
                          and  
                            $ 
                            b 
                         . This is especially the case if she knew enough about herself to know that she changed modes and that the dollar value of  
                            L 
                          to her in mode  
                            M 
                          was  
                            $ 
                            a 
                          and in mode  
                            N 
                          was  
                            $ 
                            b 
                         . In such a circumstance, her reasonable strategy would be to accept a buying price  
                            < 
                            $ 
                            a 
                          and a selling price  
                            > 
                            $ 
                            b 
                         . There is no logical necessity to have a buying price and selling prices of an object be near the utility equivalent in dollars to the utility of the object. And in this case it is economically ruinous for the decision maker to do so. Interestingly, there is an empirical literature showing that people’s buying and selling prices can differ considerably (e.g. see   Kahneman, Knetsch, & Thaler, 1990 ). 
                            5 
                         
                         
                            5 
                            The finding of a single “market price” across different modes resembles the classical economic problem of the intercomparability of utilities across people. (See  Narens & Luce, 1983 , for a measurement-theoretic account and discussion, and ( Luce, 2010 ), for a scheme for intercomparability.) The differences are: (i) The mode form is an intracomparability issue instead of a intercomparability issue. (ii) An assumption of common knowledge across modes is perhaps a more reasonable assumption for intracomparability than intercomparability. (iii) For intracomparability in many MUT models, for example, DSEU, there is across modes a common utility function  
                                  u 
                                on outcomes and a common probability function  
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                on events, whereas for intercomparability, the utility function is not common, and in some cases the probability function is not common. (iv) Intercomparability implicitly assumes common interpretations of descriptions of lotteries, whereas for intracomparability the interpretations are not common, except for degenerate cases. It appears to me that despite differences like these, the core issue concerning the validity of such comparabilities is essentially the same for modes and people.
