Many in the literature consider violations of the Sure Thing Principle to be demonstrations of irrationality, thus Case 2a is a demonstration of irrational utility calculations. Putting aside the issue of the universal irrationality of failures of the Sure Thing Principle, one can still inquire whether Case 2a is a reasonable way to include a special role for catastrophes in MUT, especially for decision makers who view it necessary to provide such a special role. The problem with Case 2a from the perspective of the Sure Thing Principle is that a description of a catastrophic outcome triggers catastrophic interpretations of lottery event descriptions that have non-catastrophic outcomes. This is the multimode mechanism that produces the violation of the Sure Thing Principle. This mechanism, while perhaps descriptively valid in some cases, appears to me to be a poor one normatively. Case 2b, avoids this problem by having each fear interpretation of a lottery event description being only invoked by a description of a catastrophic event (and its associated catastrophic outcome). From a normative point of view, Case 2b appears to me to be a better decision making approach to catastrophes than Case 2a.

Note that Case 2b does not allow for descriptions of lotteries where catastrophic events have non-catastrophic outcomes or for descriptions of lotteries where non-catastrophic events having catastrophic outcomes. This eliminates highly artificial cases such as death if a die comes up even and $1 if it comes up odd. In SEU every outcome is paired with every event in some lottery. This is a highly unrealistic assumption, but one that has powerful consequences, and some rationality arguments depend on those consequences. DSEU is designed to avoid this assumption, and in particular, DSEU allows descriptions of events and outcomes to be linked through mode. In my view, SEU’s inclusion of extraneous lotteries does not help for the development of a range of rational theories: Instead, it hurts this effort in a manner somewhat analogous to including all subsets of the infinite universal set in the domain of a probability function hurts applications that require  
                            σ 
                         -additivity.

The rationality arguments for SEU were created to show that for the phenomena SEU was designed to model, SEU was rational. They need not apply for rational decision making for other kinds of phenomena. Because of this, several of the assumptions in rationality arguments for SEU do not apply MUT and its specialized model DSEU. In particular, the following has been noted:  
                            
                               1. 
                               The event algebra of SEU and DSEU are different. The rationality arguments for SEU assume a boolean algebra of events. To my knowledge, a well-reasoned attempt to justify this assumption has never been made in the literature. This assumption fails in some DSEU models: DSEU assumes a pseudo complemented distributive algebra of events. 
                            
                            
                               2. 
                               The lotteries under consideration are different. The lottery terms in SEU consists of all possible pairs of outcome and events. This assumption, which is used in rationality arguments for SEU, is not rationally justified. It fails in some DSEU models. 
                            
                            
                               3. 
                               The rationality arguments for SEU assume an objective world and that the decision maker agrees with the objective occurrence/nonoccurence of events. This assumption fails in some DSEU models. 
                            
                            
                               4. 
                               The rationality arguments for SEU assume a decision maker who has objective interpretations of descriptions of events. This assumption fails in some DSEU models. It occur in DSEU as only as a degenerate case. 
                            
                            
                               5. 
                               The rationality arguments for SEU assume the buying and selling price of outcomes coincide. This is not assumed in all MUT situations.

The event algebra of SEU and DSEU are different. The rationality arguments for SEU assume a boolean algebra of events. To my knowledge, a well-reasoned attempt to justify this assumption has never been made in the literature. This assumption fails in some DSEU models: DSEU assumes a pseudo complemented distributive algebra of events.

The lotteries under consideration are different. The lottery terms in SEU consists of all possible pairs of outcome and events. This assumption, which is used in rationality arguments for SEU, is not rationally justified. It fails in some DSEU models.

The rationality arguments for SEU assume an objective world and that the decision maker agrees with the objective occurrence/nonoccurence of events. This assumption fails in some DSEU models.

The rationality arguments for SEU assume a decision maker who has objective interpretations of descriptions of events. This assumption fails in some DSEU models. It occur in DSEU as only as a degenerate case.

The rationality arguments for SEU assume the buying and selling price of outcomes coincide. This is not assumed in all MUT situations.

Despite these differences, from the perspective of rationality arguments for SEU, DSEU automatically retains a good deal of rationality:  Theorem 1  below shows that under standard background assumptions DSEU models are imbeddable in SEU models. This implies that much of SEU rationality transfers to DSEU.

The following definitions are needed in the formulation and proof of  Theorem 1 .

Theorem 1 
                            
                               Suppose DSEU and that  
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                
                               is the unique probability function on  
                               
                                  
                                     〈 
                                     A 
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           ≾ 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                              P 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     〉 
                                  
                                   ( 
                               
                                  Definition   1 
                               ). Then there exists a SEU model  
                               
                                  
                                     〈 
                                     
                                        
                                           E 
                                        
                                        
                                           ⋆ 
                                        
                                     
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              P 
                                           
                                        
                                        
                                           ⋆

The “standard background assumptions” for DSEU used in  Theorem 1  and referred to earlier is the uniqueness of  
                            
                               P 
                            
                          ( Definition 1 ). It is well-known in the measurement theory literature that uniqueness of a probability function for a structure like  
                            
                               〈 
                               A 
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     ≾ 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                     
                                  
                               
                               〉 
                            
                          is easy to achieve if the domain of the structure is sufficiently rich in events. It is also worthwhile to note that the probability functions that result from the standard axiomatizations of SEU are unique.

This article introduced three major changes into the formalism of the SEU theory of decision making:  
                            
                               • 
                               The introduction of modes of the decision maker. This allows for a wider range of mental activity for the decision maker while making decisions. 
                            
                            
                               • 
                               The use of descriptions of events as a primitive concept. This idea was borrowed from a descriptive, psychological theory of probability estimation (i.e., from “support theory”). It allows for events to have multiple interpretations by allowing the interpretation of the description to vary with mode. 
                            
                            
                               • 
                               The change of the event space from a boolean algebra to a pseudo complemented distributive algebra. This provides a mechanism for allowing subjective probabilities to vary with modes while maintaining coherence within and across lotteries under change in mode. One consequence of coherence that lotteries will remain lotteries under change of mode. 
                            
                          While these three changes are major innovations with respect to SEU, they essentially leave intact the ideas involved in SEU’s formula for computing the utility of lotteries. This allows DSEU to maintain a highly rational flavor in the tradition of SEU, while allowing the decision maker to fluctuate among modes during her calculations of utilities.

The introduction of modes of the decision maker. This allows for a wider range of mental activity for the decision maker while making decisions.

The use of descriptions of events as a primitive concept. This idea was borrowed from a descriptive, psychological theory of probability estimation (i.e., from “support theory”). It allows for events to have multiple interpretations by allowing the interpretation of the description to vary with mode.

The change of the event space from a boolean algebra to a pseudo complemented distributive algebra. This provides a mechanism for allowing subjective probabilities to vary with modes while maintaining coherence within and across lotteries under change in mode. One consequence of coherence that lotteries will remain lotteries under change of mode.

A main driving force for the normative features of DSEU is the assumption that it uses a probability function  
                            
                               P 
                            
                          on its event space  
                            A 
                          to compute subjective probabilities. This feature permits DSEU to have a coherent system of additive subjective probabilities, while failing in some of its models to satisfy other probabilistic forms of additivity, for example, consolidation. Because of DSEU’s changes to the formalism of SEU, various kinds of behavior that were arguably irrational for SEU are not relevant for determining the rationality/irrationality of DSEU. More generally, coherence in SEU is defined as a relationship between objective lotteries and objective behavior of the decision maker, whereas coherence in DSEU is defined as a relationship between the decision maker’s subjective interpretations of lotteries and her subjective interpretations of her behavior.  Fig. 3 
                          illustrates this.

Decision models are often classified as “descriptive” or “normative”, with normative usually being identified with “rational”. By this usage, MUT and DSEU are descriptive. In a similar vein to the quotation by Gilboa, Postlewaite, & Schmeidler at the beginning of this section, I find this classification of little value for models of human decision making, because of the important roles cognitive representation and emotion play in human decision making. And while some psychologists may designate certain human emotional states as “neutral”, these “neutral” states do not appear to have much to do with rational or normative decision making. DSEU shows how shifting affect can impact subjective probabilities while yielding coherent decision making. I conclude from this result that it is possible to include affect into normative decision making—or put differently—to redefine normative decision making to include affect. In my opinion, such an inclusion allows for a better decision theory for humans, both from normative and descriptive perspectives, than those, like SEU, that ignore affect.

Humans are also subjective. In traditional decision theories, subjectivity is treated like a crazed person in need of a straight jacket. In SEU, the straight jacket takes the form of a rigid structure that only allows subjectivity for the importance of outcomes and the size of probabilities. In contrast, MUT sees instead a colorful individual in need of the kind of clarity provided by, for example, DSEU.

This article began with an observation of  Luce (1956)  that “it is clear that the idealized theory [of economic utility theory] cannot exhibit certain possibly important phenomena. Possibly this is not a concern if all the applications were actually treated as normative, though even that is doubtful”.  Luce (1956)  broke with established economic tradition by introducing into utility theory a semiorder to order the value of objects. In later work (especially   Luce, 2000 ) he also used a qualitative primitive operation  
                            ⊕ 
                          of  joint receipt , where  
                            x 
                            ⊕ 
                            y 
                          stands for the object of value of receiving jointly the objects of value  
                            x 
                          and  
                            y 
                         . Economics, however, has been reluctant to accept utility theories based on new primitive concepts, even if such primitives express important utility concepts are not expressible in the standard theory and do not violate rationality. The reasons for this are difficult to fathom.

Luce adds structure to preference relation to achieve his representations. This article achieves its representations by generalizing the logical structure of event space. I know of no argument for the necessity of boolean event space for rational decision making. The economic models just assume it without comment, as do standard mathematical treatments of probability theory. This article generalizes the event space for probability to a pseudo complemented distributive algebra. It differs from boolean algebra in that its lacks complementation, that is, its pseudo complementation operator,  
                            
                               
                                  − 
                               
                               
                                  ̇ 
                               
                            
                         , may satisfy  
                            
                               
                                  A 
                                  ∪ 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        − 
                                     
                                     
                                        ̇ 
                                     
                                  
                                  A 
                                  
                                  ≠ 
                                    the sure event 
                               
                            
                          for some  
                            A 
                          in the algebra. As discussed below, this difference does not endanger the rationality of the probability theory built on it.

The origins of the mathematics of probability began in the Seventeenth Century with the replies by Blaise Pascal and Pierre de Fermat to an inquiry by the gambler Chevalier de Mere concerning betting strategies in a game of chance. Inherent in such games is the premise that the outcome of the gamble will be completely determined at some future time to the satisfaction of the participants. This imposes a condition called “the Law of the Excluded Middle” on the logical structure of the space of gambling events, that is, it will be determined that event on which the gamble is based has occurred or it will be determined that its complement has occurred.  Boole (1854)  formulated a logical structure for gambling events for his mathematical treatment of probability. Today this structure is called “a boolean algebra of events”. One of its conditions is the Law of the Excluded Middle. There are, however, kinds of gambling situations for which the Law of the Excluded Middle fails, e.g., when there is a disagreement about the occurrence of an event, when it can be determined if an event occurred but its non-occurrence is impossible to determine, etc. Pseudo complemented distributive lattices model such situations quite well.

The existence of plausible non-boolean probability theories does not contradict the rationality of boolean probability theory. They, however, call into question whether the only rational probability theories are boolean. For pseudo complemented distributive algebra of events there is an argument that if boolean probability theory is rational then so is pseudo complemented probability theory. The argument goes as follows.

The main non-boolean probability theory used in science is quantum probability theory from physics. Its event space consists of closed subspaces of a Hilbert space. Although it has been applied to economics and psychology, I do not know of any credible proposals, pro or con, regarding its rationality or normativeness for decision making.
